Al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī: Limiting Punishment to Disbelievers and Sinners
Sanusi describes al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī's limited affirmation of grave punishment and denial of angel names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view of al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī that the punishment of the grave applies only to disbelievers and sinners, and that the angels should not be named Munkar and Nakīr.

Core Idea
Al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī limited grave punishment but denied the angelic names.

Sanusi next reports the view of al-Balkhī, al-Jubbā'ī, and his son. They affirmed the punishment of the grave for disbelievers and sinners (fāsiqīn) but excluded believers. However, they objected to the names Munkar and Nakīr for the two angels, despite these names being mentioned in the Sacred Law. This lesson examines their partial affirmation and their denial of the angels' names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully.
Notice the verbs 'أثبت' (affirmed) and 'أنكروا' (denied).
2
Identify the affirmed and denied
They affirm punishment for kuffār and fāsiqūn, but deny the names Munkar and Nakīr. They also exclude believers.
3
Understand the denial of names
They rejected the specific names, but Sanusi counters that the Sharia has given those names.
4
Reflect on the partial affirmation
This view is closer to Sunnism than the previous ones, but still deviates on the names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نُقِلَ عن ضرار وبشرِ المَرِّيسي وجماعةٍ مِنَ المعتزلة .. إنكارُ عذاب القبر والمساءلة فيه ورد الأرواح فيه إلى الأجسادِ ، وقالوا : مَنْ ماتَ فهو ميت في قبره إلى يوم القيامة. وزعم أبو الهذيلِ مِنَ المعتزلة : أنَّ مَنْ خرجَ مِنَ الدنيا على غيرِ سمةِ الإيمانِ فإِنَّهُ يُعذِّبُ بينَ النفختين ويُسأَلُ إِذْ ذاك (۱) وأثبت البلخي والجبائي وابنه عذاب القبر للكفَّارِ والفاسقين دونَ المؤمنين ، وأنكروا تسمية الملكين بمُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرِ ، والشرع ورد بتسميتهما بذلك (٢) وقال صالح قِبَّةٌ مِنَ المعتزلة : عذاب القبر جائز ، ويجري على المؤمنينَ مِنْ غيرِ ردّ الأرواحِ إلى أجسادِها ، وقال : إنَّ الميت يجوزُ أنْ يُحِسَّ ويألم ! وهو خلافُ الضرورة . وقالَتْ طائفةٌ مِنَ الكرَّاميَّةِ والمعتزلةِ : إِنَّ اللهَ تعالى يُعذِّبُ الموتى في قبورهم ويُحدِثُ فيهمُ الألم وهم لا يشعرونَ ، فإذا أُحْيُوا وجدوا تلك الآلام ، قالوا : كالسكران إذا ضُرِبَ فَإِنَّهُ يُحِسُّ أَلمَهُ بعدُ إِذا رجعَ إليه عقله !
And it has been transmitted from Ḍirār and Bishr al-Marīsī and a group of the Mu'tazila ... the denial of the punishment of the grave and the questioning in it and the return of souls to bodies in it, and they said: Whoever dies, he is dead in his grave until the Day of Resurrection. And Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila claimed that whoever leaves the world without the mark of faith, then he will be punished between the two trumpet blasts and will be questioned then. And al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī and his son affirmed the punishment of the grave for disbelievers and sinners but not for believers, and they denied the naming of the two angels as Munkar and Nakīr, while the Sacred Law has come with their naming as such. And Ṣāliḥ Qubbah of the Mu'tazila said: The punishment of the grave is possible and it occurs to believers without returning souls to their bodies, and he said: It is possible for the dead to feel and experience pain! And that is contrary to necessity. And a group of the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila said: God Most High punishes the dead in their graves and creates in them pain while they are not aware, so when they are resurrected they find those pains. They said: Like a drunk person who is struck, then he feels his pain later when his intellect returns to him!
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage enumerates the various deviant opinions regarding the punishment of the grave. It begins with the view of Ḍirār, Bishr al-Marīsī, and a group of Mu'tazila who completely deny the punishment, questioning, and return of souls. Then it mentions Abu al-Hudhayl's view that only non-believers are punished between the two trumpet blasts. Then al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī limit the punishment to disbelievers and sinners and deny the names of the angels Munkar and Nakīr. Then Ṣāliḥ Qubbah claims it is possible without returning souls, and the dead can feel pain — which the author notes is contrary to necessity. Finally, the Karramiyya and some Mu'tazila say the punishment occurs but the dead are unaware, like a drunk person.

Key Terms
منكر ونكير
عذاب القبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The angels Munkar and Nakīr are not mentioned in the Qur'an or hadith.
Correction: Sanusi states that the Sacred Law (al-sharʿ) has confirmed their names, contrary to the denial of al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī.

Checkpoint
Which group affirmed the punishment of the grave for disbelievers and sinners but denied the names Munkar and Nakīr?
A) Ḍirār and Bishr al-Marīsī
B) Abu al-Hudhayl
C) al-Balkhī, al-Jubbā'ī, and his son
D) Ṣāliḥ Qubbah

Key Takeaway
Al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī accepted the punishment of the grave for disbelievers and sinners but wrongly objected to the names of the questioning angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī objected to the names of the angels because they seemed unreasonable, do you find yourself hesitant to accept any clear teaching of Islam because it doesn't make sense to you? What would it cost you to submit fully today?
?
The lesson shows that even those who affirm some aspects of the unseen can fall short in others. Examine your own heart: are there parts of the afterlife (like the details of the grave or the angels) that you have neglected or downplayed? How can you revive your conviction in them starting this moment?
?
Al-Balkhī and al-Jubbā'ī limited punishment to disbelievers and sinners, but the full creed warns believers too. Do you secretly believe that your faith exempts you from accountability? What sin have you been complacent about that this lesson calls you to address now?

Ṣāliḥ Qubbah and the Karramiyya on Unconscious Punishment
Sanusi reports Ṣāliḥ Qubbah's and the Karramiyya's views on unconscious punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of Ṣāliḥ Qubbah and the Karramiyya regarding the punishment of the grave, particularly the possibility of punishment without soul return and the idea of unconscious suffering.

Core Idea
Some Mu'tazila and Karramiyya said the dead can be punished without awareness.

Sanusi concludes his survey with two more positions. First, Ṣāliḥ Qubbah claimed that the punishment of the grave is possible for believers without returning souls to bodies, and that the dead can feel pain — a claim Sanusi dismisses as contrary to necessity. Then, a group of Karramiyya and Mu'tazila asserted that God inflicts pain in the dead while they are unaware, and they only feel it upon resurrection, drawing an analogy with a drunk person. This lesson analyzes these unusual theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two distinct views presented.
2
Understand Ṣāliḥ Qubbah's view
He says punishment is possible (jā'iz) for believers without return of souls, and the dead can feel pain. Sanusi calls this 'contrary to necessity'.
The phrase 'خلاف الضرورة' indicates Sanusi's strong rejection.
3
Understand the Karramiyya view
They say God creates pain in the dead but they are unaware until resurrection, like a drunk who feels pain later.
4
Compare the two views
Both involve unconsciousness, but Ṣāliḥ Qubbah's view allows immediate feeling (which Sanusi refutes), while the Karramiyya postpone awareness.

Refuting the Karramiyya Analogy: Punishment of the Grave
Sanusi refutes the Karramiyya analogy and explains rational possibility of grave punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Karramiyya analogy and establishes the rational possibility of punishment of the grave.

Core Idea
The drunk person feels pain but cannot show it; likewise the dead can experience punishment without visible signs.

Sanusi here addresses a common objection to the reality of punishment of the grave. The Karramiyya sect argued that since a drunk person feels no pain, the dead cannot feel punishment either. Sanusi and his companions refute this by pointing out that the drunk person does feel pain but is unable to express it. He then provides a rational explanation for how the deceased can perceive the angels and experience punishment, even if we do not see or hear it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's refutation and rational explanation sink in.
Notice the two distinct parts: refutation and rational proof.
2
Identify the Karramiyya analogy
The analogy compares the deceased to a drunk person who supposedly feels no pain. Understand why this analogy is flawed according to Sanusi.
3
Examine the rational possibility
Sanusi states there is no rational impediment to returning life to some parts of the deceased, granting intellect and perception. Reflect on how reason allows this.
This is a key point in defending samiyyat (matters of the unseen) through rational reasoning.
4
Connect to broader theology
This passage defends the reality of punishment of the grave, a core Sunni belief. Understand how this rational argument supports transmitted teachings.
Punishment of the grave is known through revelation, but reason shows it is not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنع أصحابنا أنَّ السكران لا يتألَّمُ ، وإِنَّما منعَهُ مِنَ الأنينِ والتأوه حاله. واعلم : أنَّهُ لا مانع في العقلِ مِنْ ردَّ الحياةِ إلى بعض أجزائِهِ . ويُجعَلَ لَهُ مِنَ العقل والفهم ما يفهم به ويجيب ، ويدركه الملكان منه ، وإن لم نسمع نحنُ كلامهم ، وكذا يجوزُ أنْ يسمع كلامَ مَنْ سلَّمَ
And our companions prevented that the drunk person does not feel pain, but rather his condition prevents him from groaning and moaning. And know: that there is no impediment in reason from returning life to some of his parts. And he is given of intellect and understanding that he understands and answers, and the two angels perceive it from him, even if we do not hear their speech, and likewise it is possible for him to hear the speech of whoever greets [him].
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage contains the refutation of the Karramiyya analogy and the author's rational proof. First, the author's companions (أصحابنا) refute the analogy of the drunk person, arguing that the drunk person does feel pain but his condition prevents him from expressing it. Then the author states that there is no rational impediment to returning life to some parts of the deceased, granting him intellect and understanding to perceive the angels and answer, even if we do not hear them. He also states that the deceased can hear the greeting of a living person.

Key Terms
عقل
الملكان
رد الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya believe that a drunk person feels no pain, so the dead similarly feel no punishment.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the drunk person does feel pain but is unable to express it; therefore the analogy fails, and the dead can indeed feel punishment.

Checkpoint

A) The drunk person feels no pain at all
B) The drunk person feels pain but his condition prevents him from showing it

D) The drunk person's pain is imaginary

Key Takeaway
Punishment of the grave is rationally possible because Allah can return life to some parts of the deceased, granting them perception, and the inability to express pain does not mean its absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Views on Punishment in the Grave (1): From Denial to Restriction
Sanusi presents two deviant Mu'tazila views on the timing and scope of punishment in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of early Mu'tazila scholars on punishment in the grave, including Abu al-Hudhayl's unique timing and al-Balkhi's restriction to disbelievers and sinners.

Core Idea
The early Mu'tazila split on punishment in the grave: some postponed it, others limited it.

Sanusi begins by stating the Sunni baseline: the dead remain in their graves until resurrection. He then records two early Mu'tazila views that deviate from this. Abu al-Hudhayl shifts punishment to the interval between the two trumpet blasts, while al-Balkhi and al-Jubba'i restrict it to non-believers and sinners, even denying the names of the questioning angels. This lesson explores these positions and highlights their divergence from the transmitted teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the baseline Sunni view
Start by reading the opening line that the dead are in their graves until resurrection. This is the orthodox starting point.
2
Examine Abu al-Hudhayl's claim
Notice that he says punishment occurs between the two trumpet blasts, not immediately after death. How does this differ from the common understanding?
3
Understand al-Balkhi and al-Jubba'i's view
They affirm punishment in the grave but only for disbelievers and sinners, not believers. Also note their denial of the angel names Munkar and Nakir.
Why would they deny the names? The text says the Shari'a explicitly named them.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider that these scholars accept punishment in the grave in some form but modify its scope or timing. What does this tell us about the early debates?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مات فهو ميت في قبره إلى يوم القيامة
وزعم أبو الهذيلِ مِنَ المعتزلة : أنَّ مَنْ خرجَ مِنَ الدنيا على غيرِ سمةِ الإيمانِ فإِنَّهُ يُعذِّبُ بينَ النفختين ويُسأَلُ إِذْ ذاك (۱)
وأثبت البلخي والجبائي وابنه عذاب القبر للكفَّارِ والفاسقين دونَ المؤمنين ، وأنكروا تسمية الملكين بمُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرِ ، والشرع ورد بتسميتهما بذلك (٢)
وقال صالح قِبَّةٌ مِنَ المعتزلة : عذاب القبر جائز ، ويجري على المؤمنينَ مِنْ غيرِ ردِّ الأرواحِ إلى أجسادِها ، وقال : إنَّ الميت يجوزُ أنْ يُحِسَّ ويألم ! وهو خلافُ الضرورة .
وقالَتْ طائفةٌ مِنَ الكرَّاميَّةِ والمعتزلةِ : إِنَّ اللهَ تعالى يُعذِّبُ الموتى في قبورهم ويُحدِثُ فيهمُ الألم وهم لا يشعرونَ ، فإذا أُحْيُوا وجدوا تلك الآلام ، قالوا : كالسكران إذا ضُرِبَ فَإِنَّهُ يُحِسُّ أَلمَهُ بعدُ إِذا رجعَ إليه عقله ! ومنع أصحابنا أنَّ السكران لا يتألَّمُ ، وإِنَّما منعَهُ مِنَ الأنينِ والتأوه حاله
He who dies is dead in his grave until the Day of Resurrection. And Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila claimed that whoever leaves this world without the mark of faith will be punished between the two trumpet blasts and will be questioned at that time. And al-Balkhi, al-Jubba'i, and his son affirmed punishment in the grave for disbelievers and sinners but not for believers, and they denied the naming of the two angels as Munkar and Nakir, while the Shari'a came with that naming. And Salih Qubba of the Mu'tazila said: Punishment in the grave is permissible, and it occurs to believers without returning spirits to their bodies, and he said: The dead may feel and suffer! And that is contrary to necessity. And a group of the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila said: God the Exalted punishes the dead in their graves and creates pain in them while they are unaware, and when they are revived they find those pains. They said: Like a drunkard when he is beaten, then he feels his pain later when his reason returns to him. And our scholars denied that the drunkard does not suffer; rather, his state prevents him from groaning and moaning.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a doxographical survey of erroneous opinions regarding punishment in the grave, followed by an implicit refutation. The author first states the baseline Sunni position: the dead remain in their graves until resurrection. Then he presents four deviant views: (1) Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila holds that those who die without faith are punished between the two trumpet blasts (the first blow and the second), and they are questioned then. (2) Al-Balkhi, al-Jubba'i, and his son affirm punishment in the grave for disbelievers and sinners but deny it for believers, and they reject the traditional names of the two angels (Munkar and Nakir), though the Shari'a explicitly names them so. (3) Salih Qubba says punishment in the grave is possible and happens to believers without the souls returning to the bodies, claiming the dead can feel pain—this is contrary to self-evident knowledge (ضرورة). (4) A group from the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila say God punishes the dead while they are unaware, but they feel the pain later when revived, analogizing to a drunkard who feels pain only after regaining consciousness. The author notes that our scholars (أصحابنا, i.e., Ash'aris) reject this analogy, affirming that the drunkard does indeed suffer but is prevented from expressing it due to his condition. Thus, these views are all refuted either by reason or by transmitted texts.

Key Terms
عذاب القبر
مُنْكَر ونَكِير
أبو الهذيل
البلخي
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Mu'tazila deny punishment in the grave entirely.
Correction: This passage shows that early Mu'tazila figures like Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Balkhi affirmed some form of punishment in the grave, though with differences in timing and scope.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what did al-Balkhi and al-Jubba'i deny regarding punishment in the grave?
A) They denied punishment in the grave entirely.
B) They denied that believers receive punishment in the grave.
C) They denied the names of the two angels Munkar and Nakir.
D) They denied that punishment occurs after death.

Key Takeaway
The early Mu'tazila accepted punishment in the grave in modified forms, showing that the doctrine was debated even among those who later denied it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that some scholars denied punishment in the grave for believers, does your heart secretly hope that your own sins will not be questioned? Will you live today as if you will face Munkar and Nakir tonight, or do you assume you are safe?
?
If the Mu'tazila could modify revelation to fit their reasoning, where in your life have you subtly adjusted Allah's commands to suit your comfort? Are you ready to submit fully, even to matters like the naming of angels that seem trivial?
?
The Shari'a explicitly names the angels, yet some denied it. Do you ever downplay explicit teachings of the Quran and Sunnah because they make you uncomfortable? What would it cost you to accept them completely starting now?

Views on Punishment in the Grave (2): Permissibility and Refutation
Sanusi presents two more deviant views on how punishment in the grave occurs and the Ash'ari refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of Salih Qubba and the Karramiyya on punishment in the grave, and the Ash'ari refutation of their rationalizations.

Core Idea
Salih Qubba and the Karramiyya proposed that the dead can feel punishment without souls or awareness, but Ash'aris refuted these claims.

Sanusi continues recording heretical views on punishment in the grave. Salih Qubba argues that punishment can occur without the return of souls, claiming the dead can feel pain. The Karramiyya and some Mu'tazila say God punishes the dead while they are unaware, and they only feel it upon revival, analogizing to a drunkard. The Ash'ari scholars reject these analogies, affirming that the dead indeed suffer but are prevented from expressing it. This lesson examines these claims and the Sunni response.

Guided Path
1
Read Salih Qubba's position
He says punishment is permissible and occurs to believers without returning souls. He claims the dead can feel and suffer.
Note that the author calls this contrary to ضرورة (self-evident knowledge).
2
Examine the Karramiyya analogy
They say God punishes the dead unaware, and they feel it later when revived. They compare this to a drunkard who feels pain after regaining consciousness.
3
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
Our scholars deny that the drunkard does not suffer; rather, his state prevents him from expressing it. Thus, the analogy fails because it assumes lack of awareness.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal stakes
Why is it important that the dead are aware of punishment? How does this impact the view of the grave as a place of reward or punishment?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مات فهو ميت في قبره إلى يوم القيامة
وزعم أبو الهذيلِ مِنَ المعتزلة : أنَّ مَنْ خرجَ مِنَ الدنيا على غيرِ سمةِ الإيمانِ فإِنَّهُ يُعذِّبُ بينَ النفختين ويُسأَلُ إِذْ ذاك (۱)
وأثبت البلخي والجبائي وابنه عذاب القبر للكفَّارِ والفاسقين دونَ المؤمنين ، وأنكروا تسمية الملكين بمُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرِ ، والشرع ورد بتسميتهما بذلك (٢)
وقال صالح قِبَّةٌ مِنَ المعتزلة : عذاب القبر جائز ، ويجري على المؤمنينَ مِنْ غيرِ ردِّ الأرواحِ إلى أجسادِها ، وقال : إنَّ الميت يجوزُ أنْ يُحِسَّ ويألم ! وهو خلافُ الضرورة .
وقالَتْ طائفةٌ مِنَ الكرَّاميَّةِ والمعتزلةِ : إِنَّ اللهَ تعالى يُعذِّبُ الموتى في قبورهم ويُحدِثُ فيهمُ الألم وهم لا يشعرونَ ، فإذا أُحْيُوا وجدوا تلك الآلام ، قالوا : كالسكران إذا ضُرِبَ فَإِنَّهُ يُحِسُّ أَلمَهُ بعدُ إِذا رجعَ إليه عقله ! ومنع أصحابنا أنَّ السكران لا يتألَّمُ ، وإِنَّما منعَهُ مِنَ الأنينِ والتأوه حاله
He who dies is dead in his grave until the Day of Resurrection. And Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila claimed that whoever leaves this world without the mark of faith will be punished between the two trumpet blasts and will be questioned at that time. And al-Balkhi, al-Jubba'i, and his son affirmed punishment in the grave for disbelievers and sinners but not for believers, and they denied the naming of the two angels as Munkar and Nakir, while the Shari'a came with that naming. And Salih Qubba of the Mu'tazila said: Punishment in the grave is permissible, and it occurs to believers without returning spirits to their bodies, and he said: The dead may feel and suffer! And that is contrary to necessity. And a group of the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila said: God the Exalted punishes the dead in their graves and creates pain in them while they are unaware, and when they are revived they find those pains. They said: Like a drunkard when he is beaten, then he feels his pain later when his reason returns to him. And our scholars denied that the drunkard does not suffer; rather, his state prevents him from groaning and moaning.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a doxographical survey of erroneous opinions regarding punishment in the grave, followed by an implicit refutation. The author first states the baseline Sunni position: the dead remain in their graves until resurrection. Then he presents four deviant views: (1) Abu al-Hudhayl of the Mu'tazila holds that those who die without faith are punished between the two trumpet blasts (the first blow and the second), and they are questioned then. (2) Al-Balkhi, al-Jubba'i, and his son affirm punishment in the grave for disbelievers and sinners but deny it for believers, and they reject the traditional names of the two angels (Munkar and Nakir), though the Shari'a explicitly names them so. (3) Salih Qubba says punishment in the grave is possible and happens to believers without the souls returning to the bodies, claiming the dead can feel pain—this is contrary to self-evident knowledge (ضرورة). (4) A group from the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila say God punishes the dead while they are unaware, but they feel the pain later when revived, analogizing to a drunkard who feels pain only after regaining consciousness. The author notes that our scholars (أصحابنا, i.e., Ash'aris) reject this analogy, affirming that the drunkard does indeed suffer but is prevented from expressing it due to his condition. Thus, these views are all refuted either by reason or by transmitted texts.

Key Terms
صالح قبة
الكرامية
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the dead cannot feel anything at all in the grave.
Correction: Sunni doctrine holds that the dead do feel punishment and reward, as indicated by hadith and rational arguments, though the exact mode is unknown.

Checkpoint
How did the Ash'ari scholars respond to the Karramiyya's analogy of the drunkard?
A) They accepted the analogy.
B) They denied that the drunkard feels pain.
C) They said the drunkard does feel pain but is prevented from expressing it.
D) They said the dead are unaware of punishment.

Key Takeaway
The dead are aware of punishment in the grave, contrary to claims that they are unaware, as the Ash'ari scholars refuted the drunkard analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you, like the drunkard, numbing yourself with distractions so you ignore the spiritual pain of your sins, pretending not to feel them until it's too late? What can you do today to stop avoiding that awareness?
?
The Karramiyya believed punishment could happen without you knowing it. In your daily life, do you act as if your wrong actions have no immediate effect on your soul? How does this passage force you to reconsider that assumption?
?
Salih Qubba thought the body could be punished without the spirit. Do you sometimes separate your outward behavior from your inner state, thinking you can sin without harming your soul? Will you confess one specific sin you've been ignoring and seek forgiveness today?

Children and Infallibles in Grave Questioning
Sanusi discusses the lack of definitive evidence for reviving children and the case of infallibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that there is no definitive evidence for the revival of children in graves for questioning, and how the infallibles are treated differently.

Core Idea
The revival of children in graves lacks certain proof, but the general reports imply their questioning.

This lesson addresses a subtle point in eschatology: whether children who die before puberty are revived in their graves to be questioned about the covenant. The scholars note that there is no definitive transmitted report on this, though the apparent meaning of the texts suggests generality. We will also consider the case of prophets and other infallibles, who are questioned not for testing but for confirming their rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms like 'خبر مقطوع به' (definitive report) and 'تعميم' (generality).
The passage begins with 'قالوا' indicating a scholarly consensus or majority view.
2
Identify the main claim
The first part states there is no definitive report about reviving children. This means the issue is not settled by certain textual evidence, but the apparent meaning of the reports indicates everyone is questioned.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ليس في إحياء الأطفال خبر مقطوع به'.
3
Understand the condition for children
The passage then says that if children are revived, their understanding must be perfected so they can recognize their state of happiness or misery. This shows that the questioning would be meaningful only if they can comprehend.
The phrase 'تكميل فهمهم' is crucial.
4
Apply to the infallibles
Similarly, those infallible from sins (prophets) are also mentioned. Their questioning is not for trial but as a definition of their happiness, confirming their rank.
Note the phrase 'ويكون تعريفاً لسعادتهم'.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that eschatological events are not arbitrary but serve divine wisdom. The lack of definitive evidence leaves room for scholarly debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا(۲) : وليسَ في إحياء الأطفالِ (۳) خبر مقطوع به ، وظاهرُ الخبر يدلُّ على التعميم (٤) ، إلا أنَّه لا بد في ذلكَ مِنْ تكميل فهمهم ؛ ليعرفوا بذلك سعادتهم وشقاوتهم ، وكذا المعصومونَ مِنَ الذنوب (٥) ، ويكون تعريفاً لسعادتهم .
وقيل في قولِهِ تعالى : ( رَبَّنَا أَمَتَنَا أَثْنينِ وَأَحْيِيتَنَا اثْنَتَيْنِ) [غافر : ١١] : إنَّ إحدى الحياتين حياة القبرِ ، وأُورِدَ عليهِ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ تكونَ ثلاثاً (٦) .
They said: There is no definitive report regarding the revival of children (in their graves). And the apparent meaning of the report indicates generality (that all are questioned), except that it is necessary in that to complete their understanding, so that they may know by that their happiness and misery. Likewise, those infallible from sins. And it would be a definition of their happiness. And it was said concerning His statement the Exalted: 'Our Lord, You made us die twice and gave us life twice' [Ghafir 40:11]: That one of the two lives is the life of the grave. And it was objected to him: That it would then be three.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the questioning of children and prophets in the grave, and the interpretation of a specific verse. First, the scholars (قالوا) state that there is no certain transmitted report (خبر مقطوع به) regarding the reviving of children (i.e., those who die before puberty) in their graves for the purpose of being questioned about the covenant (الميثاق). However, the apparent meaning of the reports is general (تعميم) and would include children. But they note that if children are revived, their understanding must be perfected so they can recognize their state of felicity or misery. The same applies to those infallible from sins (المعصومون من الذنوب), i.e., prophets, though there is a difference of opinion—the accepted view is that they are not questioned. The second part mentions the verse "رَبَّنَا أَمَتَّنَا اثْنَتَيْنِ وَأَحْيَيْتَنَا اثْنَتَيْنِ" (Our Lord, You made us die twice and gave us life twice). Some interpreted one of the two lives as the life of the grave (i.e., the intermediate life after death before resurrection). But an objection is raised: if the life of the grave is counted, then there would be three lives (worldly life, grave life, and afterlife life), not two. This objection is noted without resolution, leaving the interpretation problematic.

Key Terms
إحياء الأطفال
المعصومون من الذنوب
خبر مقطوع به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the questioning of children in the grave is definitively established by textual evidence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that there is no definitive report (khabar maqṭū‘ bihi) on this issue; the evidence is not certain.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the revival of children in graves?
A) It is definitively established by a report.
B) There is no definitive report about it.
C) It is rejected by all scholars.
D) It only applies to infallibles.

Key Takeaway
The revival of children in graves for questioning is not definitively established, but if it occurs, their understanding must be completed so they can know their fate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the prophets are questioned in the grave simply to define their happiness, what does that say about the seriousness of the grave moment for you? How would you live differently if you truly believed that you will be questioned—not to learn your fate, but to confirm what you already earned? Commit to a specific action tonight.
?
Consider that children have their understanding completed at that moment so they can know their state. If you die without having completed your own understanding of your faith, what will you be able to comprehend at that moment? What are you doing today to ensure you understand your religion now?
?
The passage says there is no certain report on children's revival, yet the apparent meaning implies generality. How does living with that uncertainty—knowing that there is no definitive proof but the weight of evidence points to being questioned—affect your reliance on explicit texts? Are you content with the apparent meanings of revelation, or do you seek certainty before acting? Pledge one aspect of your deen where you will act on the apparent meaning without demanding absolute certainty.

Two Deaths and Two Lives: Interpretation and Objection
Sanusi presents an interpretation of verse 40:11 and the objection that it would imply three lives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proposed interpretation of verse 40:11 regarding two deaths and two lives, and the logical objection that it would imply three lives.

Core Idea
Interpreting one of the two lives as the grave life leads to a contradiction: three lives instead of two.

This lesson examines a specific interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Our Lord, You made us die twice and gave us life twice' (Ghafir 40:11). Some scholars proposed that one of the two lives refers to the life of the grave (barzakh). However, an objection is raised: if the grave life is included, the verse would imply three lives, not two. We will analyze this objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the interpretation
First, read the Arabic verse and the proposed interpretation. The verse speaks of two deaths and two lives. Some said one of the lives is the life of the grave.
The verse is from Surah Ghafir, verse 11.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that if the grave life is one of the two, then there would be three lives: this worldly life, the grave life, and the afterlife. The verse mentions only two, so the interpretation is problematic.
The phrase 'يلزم أن تكون ثلاثاً' is the key objection.
3
Consider the logical force
Reflect on why this objection matters: it shows that interpretations must be consistent with the explicit wording of the Quran. The grave life is an intermediate state, but it is not counted as a distinct life in this verse.
4
Think about alternative views
The passage does not resolve the issue but leaves it open. Consider how other scholars might interpret the verse without the grave life.
For example, the two deaths might refer to the end of worldly life and the death after the blowing of the Trumpet, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Rule on Apparent Meaning of Texts about the Grave and the Revival of Children and Prophets
General rule for accepting revealed texts about the grave and its application to children and prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of accepting the apparent meaning of revealed texts about the grave and its specific application to the revival of children and prophets for questioning.

Core Idea
What revelation says about the unseen must be accepted literally, without forced interpretation.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a key methodological rule for dealing with reports about matters of the unseen (sam'iyyat). When the revelation speaks about events in the grave or the afterlife, we are not to impose our own rational interpretations; rather, we accept the apparent meaning as true. The passage then applies this rule to the specific question of whether children and prophets are revived in the grave for questioning, showing how scholars reconcile general reports with necessary conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the flow from a general rule to a specific application.
2
Identify the general rule
Find the phrase that states the obligation to believe the apparent meaning of revealed texts about the grave without forced interpretation.
The text says: 'فوجب اعتقاد ظاهره، ولا حاجة إلى تكلف تأويله'.
3
Examine the application to children
Note what the scholars say about the revival of children: there is no definitive text, but the apparent meaning implies generality, with a condition of completed understanding.
The condition: 'تكميل فهمهم' so they can know their happiness or misery.
4
Apply the same to prophets
The passage says 'وكذا المعصومون من الذنوب' — the same rule applies to the sinless (prophets), and it serves as a definition of their happiness.
This shows inclusivity.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the rule is important: it preserves the literal meaning of revelation and avoids speculative interpretation.
The basis is 'والله تعالى على كل شيء قدير'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه ، وكلُّ ذلك جائز ، وقد وردَ السمعُ بهِ فوجب اعتقاد ظاهره ، ولا حاجة إلى تكلُّفِ تأويله ، والله تعالى على كلِّ شيءٍ قدير . قالوا : وليسَ في إحياء الأطفالِ خبر مقطوع به ، وظاهرُ الخبر يدلُّ على التعميم ، إلا أنَّه لا بد في ذلكَ مِنْ تكميل فهمهم ؛ ليعرفوا بذلك سعادتهم وشقاوتهم ، وكذا المعصومونَ مِنَ الذنوب ، ويكون تعريفاً لسعادتهم .
All of that is permissible, and the revealed text has come with it, so it is obligatory to believe its apparent meaning, and there is no need to resort to forced interpretation. And Allah is powerful over everything. They said: There is no definitive report regarding the reviving of children, and the apparent meaning of the report indicates generality, except that it is necessary in that to complete their understanding, so that they may thereby know their happiness and misery, and likewise the sinless ones (al-ma'sumun), and it is a definition of their happiness.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage begins with a general doctrinal rule: any matter that has been reported through revelation (sam') concerning the affairs of the grave — such as the dead hearing, answering, etc. — is to be accepted in its literal sense (zahir). Forced interpretation (ta'wil) is not required, and indeed is to be avoided. The passage then applies this rule to a specific case: the revival of children in the grave for the purpose of being questioned about the covenant. The scholars ('ulama') note that there is no decisive text (khabr maqtu' bihi) on this, but the apparent meaning of the relevant reports suggests generality, i.e., that all who die, even children, are revived. However, they add a necessary condition: that their understanding be completed (takmil fahmihim) so that they can know their happiness or misery. The same applies to the sinless (al-ma'sumun), i.e., the prophets, who are also questioned. Thus, the revival is a means of informing them of their ultimate fate.

Key Terms
سمع
إحياء الأطفال
المعصومون
تأويل
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only adults who die are questioned in the grave; children and prophets are exempt.
Correction: The passage states that the apparent meaning of reports includes children and prophets, with the condition that their understanding is completed so they can know their fate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the apparent meaning of revealed texts regarding the grave?
A) They must be interpreted allegorically.
B) They must be believed in their apparent meaning without forced interpretation.
C) They are not binding.
D) Only scholars can decide.

Key Takeaway
Revealed texts about the grave are to be accepted literally, and this includes the revival of children and prophets, with the condition that their understanding is completed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses or hadith about the grave that seem difficult to accept, do you immediately trust their apparent meaning, or do you feel the urge to reinterpret them? What does that reveal about your heart's submission to Allah's words?
?
The children's understanding is completed so they can know their fate. If you were to stand in the grave tonight, would your own understanding of your deeds be enough to bring you happiness or misery? What would you change starting now?
?
You claim to believe in the unseen, but do you live as if the questioning in the grave is a certainty? If you truly believed it, what would you do differently tomorrow?

Objection from Ghafir 11 and Its Answer
Sanusi addresses the objection that interpreting one life as the grave life implies three lives, and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes an objection against the life of the grave based on a Quranic verse, using the concepts of weak implication (mafhum) and definitive proof (qati').

Core Idea
A weak implication from a verse cannot override a clear definitive proof.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a specific objection: the verse mentioning two lives seems to exclude a third, which would contradict the doctrine of life in the grave. He provides a two-part answer, first challenging the strength of the implication used in the objection, and then offering an alternative interpretation of the verse. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: if one of the two lives is the grave life, then there would be three lives. Notice the assumption that the verse implies the negation of a third life.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
Sanusi says the negation of the third is based on 'mafhum' (implication), which is weak. Learn why this is weak and how it is overruled by definitive proof.
The definitive proof (qati') likely refers to clear texts affirming the life of the grave.
3
Analyze the second part of the answer
Sanusi suggests an alternative: the verse only mentions the two lives after death because the first life (worldly life) is obvious. See how this interpretation avoids implying a third life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل في قولِهِ تعالى : ( رَبَّنَا أَمَتَنَا أَثْنينِ وَأَحْيِيتَنَا اثْنَتَيْنِ) [غافر : ١١] : إنَّ إحدى الحياتين حياة القبرِ ، وأُورِدَ عليهِ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ تكونَ ثلاثاً . وأُجيبَ : أَنَّ نفي الثالثةِ إنَّما هو بطريق المفهوم ، وهو ضعيف فيسقط ؛ لمعارضة القاطع ، ويحتمل أَنَّهُ إِنَّما خص الحياتينِ بالذِّكرِ لأنَّهما اللتان أنكروهما بعد الموتِ ، أمَّا الحياةُ الأُولى فمحسوسة ، فلا يُحتاج إلى النص عليها
And it was said regarding His statement: 'Our Lord, You have given us death twice and given us life twice' [Ghafir 11]: that one of the two lives is the life of the grave, and it was objected against it: that it would necessitate that there be three. And it was answered: that the negation of the third is only by way of implication (al-mafhum), and it is weak, so it falls due to opposition by a definitive [proof] (al-qati'). And it is possible that He only specified the two lives in mention because they are the two that are denied after death. As for the first life, it is perceived (mahsusa), so there is no need for textual evidence for it.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam-style objection and answer. Some scholars interpreted the Qur'anic verse 'Our Lord, You have given us death twice and given us life twice' as evidence that one of the two lives is the life of the grave (hayat al-qabr). The objection raised is that this interpretation would require there to be three lives: the worldly life, life in the grave, and life after resurrection. The answer is given in two parts. First, the claim that the verse implies a negation of a third life is based on what is called 'al-mafhum' (implication or concept), which is a weak form of inference in Islamic legal theory. Since it conflicts with a definitive proof (al-qati') — presumably the many texts affirming life in the grave — it is invalid. Second, it is possible that the verse only mentions two lives because the first life (worldly life) is obvious and perceived by the senses (mahsusa), so there is no need to explicitly mention it. The verse focuses on the two lives that are denied by the disbelievers: the life after death (resurrection) and the life in between (barzakh). Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حياة القبر
المفهوم
القاطع
الحياة الأولى
محسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Our Lord, You have given us death twice...' excludes the possibility of a life in the grave.
Correction: The verse does not negate the life of the grave; the negation based on implication is weak and overruled by definitive proofs, and the verse focuses on the two lives after the first obvious life.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi answer the objection that interpreting 'one of the two lives' as the grave life would imply a third life?
A) He denies that there is a third life.
B) He says the negation of the third is based on a weak implication (mafhum) and is overruled by definitive proof.
C) He reinterprets the verse to mean something else.
D) He says the third life is actually the life of the grave itself.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the objection by showing that the negation of a third life relies on a weak implication and by noting that the first life is obvious, so the verse only mentions two lives after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Our Lord, You have given us death twice and given us life twice,' do you take it as a definitive statement limiting your understanding of the afterlife? How does Sanusi's explanation challenge your assumption about the number of lives? Will you now be more cautious in deriving rulings from implied meanings?
?
Sanusi shows that a weak implication cannot stand against a definitive proof. Where in your own religious practice do you rely on weak implications instead of clear proofs? How will you change that?
?
The first life is self-evident, but do you live as if you truly believe in the two lives after death? What would change if you were fully convinced of the life of the grave and the resurrection?

The Objection from the Verse on First Death and Its Answer
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection from the verse that seems to deny a second death, explaining it refers to agonies in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite objection from the verse about not tasting death except the first death, by clarifying that it refers to agonies of death, not the event itself.

Core Idea
The verse negates agony, not the occurrence of death in the grave.

The Mu'tazila used a Qur'anic verse to argue that there is no life in the grave because that would require a second death. Sanusi's answer is subtle: the verse is about tasting the agonies of death, not about the fact of death itself. This lesson unpacks his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the verse citation and Sanusi's response.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazila hold that the verse proves there is only one death, so no life in the grave. Identify how they use the verse.
3
Understand Sanusi's distinction
Sanusi says the verse negates 'tasting the agonies of death' (ghusas), not death itself. Grasp this distinction.
The key term 'غصص الموت' (agonies of death) is central.
4
See why the 'first death' is specified
The specification of 'except the first death' is because only the first death involves agony; subsequent deaths (if any) do not.
This preserves the possibility of an intermediate death without agony.
5
Connect to the broader issue
Reflect on how this answer supports the doctrine of life in the grave (barzakh) against the Mu'tazilite denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يَذُوقُونَ فِيهَا الْمَوْتَ إِلَّا المَوْتَةَ الأُولَى ) ﴾ .. قلنا : المنفى أن يذوقوا في الجنَّةِ غُصَصَ الموتِ التي لم تثبت إلا للأُولى ، فمِنْ ثَمَّ خُصَّتْ بالذِّكرِ
If they hold to His statement: 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' [al-Dukhan 56], we say: What is negated is that they taste the agonies (ghusas) of death in Paradise, which are only confirmed for the first death. That is why it was specified in mention.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage addresses another objection from the Mu'tazila, who used the Qur'anic verse 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' to argue that there is no life in the grave because that would require a second death. Sanusi's answer is that the verse is not about the fact of death itself, but about the agonies (ghusas) of death. It means that in Paradise, the believers will not experience the painful agonies of death — they only experienced that with the first death in the world. The phrase 'except the first death' specifies the only death that involved agony; the death that occurs in the grave (if any) is not a death of agony. Thus the verse does not negate an intermediate life in the grave. The specification of 'first death' is because only the first death involves the pangs of death.

Key Terms
ذوق الموت
غصص الموت
الموتة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves there is no death after the first, so there cannot be life in the grave.
Correction: The verse only negates tasting the agonies of death in Paradise; the first death is the only one with agony, so subsequent deaths (e.g., in the grave) are possible without contradicting the verse.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the verse 'لا يذوقون فيها الموت إلا الموتة الأولى' (They will not taste death therein except the first death) actually negate?
A) The occurrence of any death after the first.
B) The tasting of the agonies of death in Paradise.
C) The possibility of life after death.
D) The resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The verse denies the experience of death's agony in Paradise, not the event of death itself, leaving room for an intermediate life in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila rejected the grave's life because they misread a single word. In what areas of your faith might you be misreading or missing the deeper meaning of Allah's words?
?
You will taste death only once with pain. After that, what will your state be? Are you living today as if you will face a second death or as if you are heading straight to the first and final appointment?

Answering the Objection from 'You Cannot Make the Dead Hear'
Sanusi responds to the verse objection, restricting its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi restricts the verse 'You cannot make the dead hear' to the worldly state, preserving the reality of the grave's questioning.

Core Idea
The dead do hear in the barzakh, but not in their worldly death.

The Mu'tazila argued that the dead cannot hear anything, using the Quranic verse 'You cannot make the dead hear.' Sanusi responds by clarifying that the verse refers to the state of being dead in this world, not the state after burial. This lesson unpacks that concise but powerful answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the concise objection and answer.
2
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila use the verse to claim the dead have no perception, contradicting the hadiths on grave questioning.
3
Analyze the answer
Sanusi restricts the verse to the worldly state ('as long as they are dead'), meaning it does not apply to the barzakh.
The key phrase is 'ما داموا موتى'.
4
Apply the principle
Recognize that scriptural verses must be interpreted in their context and not overgeneralized.
This is a common method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ( إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْنَ ) .. قلنا : المراد ما داموا موتى .
And if they hold to His statement: 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' [al-Naml 80], we say: The intended meaning is as long as they are dead.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This is a brief objection and answer. The Mu'tazila use the verse 'You cannot make the dead hear' to argue that the dead do not hear anything, including the questioning in the grave. Sanusi responds by restricting the verse: it means they do not hear while they are in a state of death (i.e., in this worldly death, before the reviving in the grave). It does not negate that after they are revived for the questioning, they can hear. The verse is about the common experience that dead bodies do not hear; it does not address the special state of the barzakh.

Key Terms
إسماع الموتى
ما داموا موتى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'You cannot make the dead hear' proves the dead have no perception whatsoever after death.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies the verse is about the dead in this worldly state, not after the revival in the grave.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi interpret the verse 'إنك لا تسمع الموتى' (You cannot make the dead hear)?
A) It means the dead never hear anything after death.
B) It means they do not hear while they are dead in this world.
C) It is abrogated.
D) It applies only to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that a verse can be restricted in meaning to preserve other revealed truths about the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Sensory Objection to Life in the Grave
Sanusi refutes the empirical objection that dead bodies remain inert, using analogies from angels and Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the sensory objection against life in the grave by appealing to unseen realities of faith.

Core Idea
Sensory observation cannot disprove unseen realities of the barzakh.

This lesson examines a common objection: we do not see the dead coming back to life, so how can they be alive? Sanusi’s response is sharp—he does not provide a physical explanation but instead reorients the question from empirical evidence to the realm of faith. He compares this objection to the disbelievers' denial of resurrection and draws analogies from accepted teachings about angels and Iblis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the objection is stated directly and how Sanusi immediately characterizes it as a sign of weak faith.
2
Understand the comparison to disbelievers
Sanusi compares the objection to the disbelievers' denial of the resurrection of decayed bones. This shows that the objection is not new but similar to a rejection of divine power.
3
Analyze the three analogies
Each analogy (Prophet seeing angels, angels rotating, Iblis seeing humans) illustrates that unseen realities exist. Reflect on how each supports the point that absence of sensory evidence does not mean non-existence.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
The key is that barzakh life is an unseen reality. Sensory observation is not the criterion for truth in matters of faith. Ask yourself: what does this imply about how we approach theological claims?
This connects to the broader method of kalam: using reason and revelation, not crude empiricism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : نحن نرى مَنْ دفناهُ على حاله ، ونعلم بالضرورة كونه ميتاً . قلنا : هذا يُؤذِنُ مِنْ قائِلِهِ بعدم طمأنينته إلى الإيمان ! وهو بمثابة استبعاد الكفرة حشْرَ العظام الباليةِ ، ومَنْ يُسلَّمُ اختصاص الرسولِ برؤية الملكِ دونَ القوم ، وتعاقب الملائكة فينا ، وقوله تعالى في إبليس وجنوده : ﴿ إِنَّهُ يَرَنكُمْ هُوَ وَقَبِيلُهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ لا ترونهم ﴾ .
If they say: We see the one we buried in his state, and we know by necessity that he is dead. We say: This indicates from its speaker a lack of tranquility in faith! And it is like the disbelievers' denial of the resurrection of decayed bones. And [we accept] the specific privilege of the Prophet in seeing the angel while others do not, and the rotation of angels among us, and His statement about Iblis and his hosts: 'Indeed, he sees you, he and his tribe, from where you do not see them.'
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage addresses a final objection based on direct observation: we bury a dead person and he remains in his state, so he cannot be alive. Sanusi's response is sharp: such an objection reveals a lack of firmness in faith (tuma'nina ila al-iman). It is analogous to the disbelievers' denial of the resurrection of decayed bones, which is a rejection of Allah's power. He then draws analogies from accepted beliefs: the Prophet's privilege of seeing the angel Jibril when others do not; the rotation of angels among us (i.e., angels come and go, seen by some but not others); and the verse about Iblis and his hosts seeing humans while humans do not see them. All these show that there are unseen realities that are not perceptible by the senses. Similarly, life in the grave is an unseen reality that cannot be disproved by sensory observation alone.

Key Terms
طمأنينة الإيمان
حشر العظام البالية
اختصاص الرسول
الملك
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a dead body remains unchanged, then there is no life in the grave.
Correction: The life of the barzakh is an unseen reality that cannot be proven or disproven by sensory observation; many unseen realities (angels, Iblis) exist despite not being perceived.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi respond to the objection that dead bodies remain unchanged and thus cannot be alive?
A) He argues that the dead body is actually alive.
B) He says this shows a lack of faith and compares it to the disbelievers' denial of resurrection.
C) He agrees and changes the topic.
D) He says the verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Sensory evidence is insufficient to disprove the unseen realities of the barzakh; faith requires accepting truths beyond empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you stand at a grave, do you feel the weight that the person buried there is alive in a way you cannot see? Or does the stillness of the body make you doubt? What does Sanusi's rebuke—that such doubt reveals a lack of tranquility in faith—say about your own heart? Will you ask Allah tonight to increase your certainty in the unseen?
?
You believe in angels you cannot see, and you accept that Iblis sees you while you do not see him. Why then do you struggle to believe that the dead are alive in the barzakh? Is it because you trust your senses more than you trust Allah's word? What specific step will you take today to align your conviction with what Allah has revealed?
?
Sanusi compares the one who denies life in the grave to the disbelievers who mocked the resurrection of decayed bones. Have you ever dismissed a truth of the unseen because it didn't fit your experience? If you truly internalised that the barzakh is real, how would it change the way you live your next twenty-four hours?

Refuting the Objection from the Verse on the First Death
Sanusi refutes the objection from Q. 44:56 about the first death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the scriptural objection that the verse 'They will not taste death except the first death' negates life in the grave.

Core Idea
Weak implication cannot override definitive proofs of life after death.

Sanusi begins by establishing a key principle: a weak linguistic implication (mufhum) cannot stand against a definitive proof (qati'). He then applies this to a Quranic verse that seems to limit death to only one occurrence, showing that the verse actually refers to the agonies of death, not to existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of mufhum
Read the first part where Sanusi explains that the negation of a third life is based on a weak implication, which falls away when opposed by a definitive proof.
This is a foundational rule in Islamic legal theory applied here to theology.
2
Examine the alternative explanation
Notice that Sanusi offers another possibility: the verse specifies two lives because they are the ones denied after death; the first life is obvious.
3
Read the objection and answer
Read the verse cited and Sanusi's response: the negation is of tasting agonies, not of a second life.
The verse is from Surah ad-Dukhan (44:56).
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the weak implication is overruled and the verse is interpreted in harmony with the doctrine of life in the grave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُجيبَ : أَنَّ نفي الثالثةِ إنَّما هو بطريق المفهوم ، وهو ضعيف فيسقط ؛ لمعارضة القاطع ، ويحتمل أَنَّهُ إِنَّما خص الحياتينِ بالذَّكرِ لأنَّهما اللتان أنكروهما بعد الموتِ ، أمَّا الحياةُ الأُولى فمحسوسة ، فلا يُحتاج إلى النص عليها فإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يَذُوقُونَ فِيهَا الْمَوْتَ إِلَّا الْمَوْتَةَ الْأُولَى ﴾ [الدخان : ٥٦] .. قلنا : المنفى أن يذوقوا في الجنَّةِ غُصَصَ الموتِ التي لم تثبت إلا للأُولى ، فمِنْ ثَمَّ خُصَّتْ بالذَّكرِ وإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْتَى ﴾ [النمل : ٨٠] .. قلنا : المراد ما داموا موتى .
And it was answered: The negation of the third [life] is only by way of implication (mufhum), which is weak and thus falls away due to being opposed by a definitive [proof]. And it is possible that He only specified the two lives because they are the ones they denied after death; as for the first life, it is perceptible, so there is no need for textual specification. If they hold fast to His statement (Exalted): 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' [Q. 44:56] ... We say: What is negated is that they taste in Paradise the agonies of death which are only established for the first [death]; for that reason it was specifically mentioned. And if they hold fast to His statement (Exalted): 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' [Q. 27:80] ... We say: The intended meaning is as long as they are dead.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from scriptural verses against the doctrine of life in the grave (barzakh) and their refutations. The first objection is based on the verse 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' from Surah ad-Dukhan. The objectors argue that this implies only one death, negating a second life after death for questioning. The answer is twofold: First, the negation of a third [life] is based on a weak linguistic implication (mufhum) which is overruled by definitive proofs of the afterlife. Second, the verse specifically mentions two lives because those are the ones they denied after death; the first life is evident through the senses, so it needed no mention. The second objection uses the verse 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' from Surah an-Naml, which seems to deny that the dead can hear. The response clarifies that this refers to the state of being dead in the worldly sense, not to the life of the barzakh where hearing is possible.

Key Terms
مفهوم (mufhum)
الموتة الأولى (the first death)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'They will not taste death except the first death' proves there is no life after death.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the negation refers to the agonies of death, not to existence itself, and that the first death is specified because it is perceptible, while the two denied lives are the ones they doubted.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute the objection based on the verse 'They will not taste death therein except the first death'?
A) By saying that the negation is based on a weak implication and the verse specifies only the first death because the first life is perceptible.
B) By denying that there is any life after death.
C) By claiming the verse is abrogated.
D) By interpreting 'death' as spiritual death.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that a weak linguistic implication cannot override definitive proofs, and the verse about the first death actually refers to agonies, not to the reality of life in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi shows that even a verse of the Quran, when misinterpreted, can become a tool of doubt. Which verses do you hold opinions about that you have never carefully studied? Will you commit to examining them with the help of credible scholars before letting them shape your belief?
?
The objection relied on a weak implication. Are there areas in your own faith where you have allowed a weak doubt—one that contradicts clear proofs—to take root? What would it cost you to uproot it today?
?
The verse speaks of tasting death only once. If you truly believed that death is a single door to an eternal reality, how would your daily priorities change? What is stopping you from living that conviction right now?

Refuting the Objection from the Verse on Not Hearing the Dead
Sanusi refutes the objection from Q. 27:80 about not hearing the dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the verse 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' denies the possibility of hearing in the grave.

Core Idea
The verse refers to worldly death, not the life of the barzakh.

The second objection quotes a verse that seems to say the dead cannot hear, which would contradict the idea of life in the grave where the dead hear questions. Sanusi provides a concise answer: the verse applies to the state of being dead in this world, not to the intermediate state (barzakh).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the verse cited and Sanusi's brief response: 'The intended meaning is as long as they are dead.'
The verse is from Surah an-Naml (27:80).
2
Understand the distinction
Recognize that 'dead' here refers to those who are physically dead and not in the barzakh life; the barzakh is a different realm where hearing is possible.
This distinction is crucial for the doctrine of life in the grave.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how Sanusi's response preserves the possibility of post-death hearing while acknowledging the verse's general sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأُجيبَ : أَنَّ نفي الثالثةِ إنَّما هو بطريق المفهوم ، وهو ضعيف فيسقط ؛ لمعارضة القاطع ، ويحتمل أَنَّهُ إِنَّما خص الحياتينِ بالذَّكرِ لأنَّهما اللتان أنكروهما بعد الموتِ ، أمَّا الحياةُ الأُولى فمحسوسة ، فلا يُحتاج إلى النص عليها فإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يَذُوقُونَ فِيهَا الْمَوْتَ إِلَّا الْمَوْتَةَ الْأُولَى ﴾ [الدخان : ٥٦] .. قلنا : المنفى أن يذوقوا في الجنَّةِ غُصَصَ الموتِ التي لم تثبت إلا للأُولى ، فمِنْ ثَمَّ خُصَّتْ بالذَّكرِ وإن تمسكوا بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّكَ لَا تُسْمِعُ الْمَوْتَى ﴾ [النمل : ٨٠] .. قلنا : المراد ما داموا موتى .
And it was answered: The negation of the third [life] is only by way of implication (mufhum), which is weak and thus falls away due to being opposed by a definitive [proof]. And it is possible that He only specified the two lives because they are the ones they denied after death; as for the first life, it is perceptible, so there is no need for textual specification. If they hold fast to His statement (Exalted): 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' [Q. 44:56] ... We say: What is negated is that they taste in Paradise the agonies of death which are only established for the first [death]; for that reason it was specifically mentioned. And if they hold fast to His statement (Exalted): 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' [Q. 27:80] ... We say: The intended meaning is as long as they are dead.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from scriptural verses against the doctrine of life in the grave (barzakh) and their refutations. The first objection is based on the verse 'They will not taste death therein except the first death' from Surah ad-Dukhan. The objectors argue that this implies only one death, negating a second life after death for questioning. The answer is twofold: First, the negation of a third [life] is based on a weak linguistic implication (mufhum) which is overruled by definitive proofs of the afterlife. Second, the verse specifically mentions two lives because those are the ones they denied after death; the first life is evident through the senses, so it needed no mention. The second objection uses the verse 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' from Surah an-Naml, which seems to deny that the dead can hear. The response clarifies that this refers to the state of being dead in the worldly sense, not to the life of the barzakh where hearing is possible.

Key Terms
الموتة الأولى (the first death)
البرزخ (barzakh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear' proves that the dead cannot hear anything after death.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the verse refers to the state of worldly death, not to the life of the barzakh where hearing is possible.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi interpret the verse 'Indeed, you cannot make the dead hear'?
A) It means the dead cannot hear at all, even in the barzakh.
B) It applies only as long as they are dead (in this world), not in the afterlife.
C) It is abrogated by other verses.
D) It refers to spiritual deafness.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi restricts the verse about not hearing the dead to the state of worldly death, preserving the possibility of hearing in the life of the barzakh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you misinterpreted a Quranic verse because you took it out of context. How does Sanusi's precise restriction of this verse challenge you to be more careful with Quranic interpretation? What step will you take today to ensure you understand verses in their full context?
?
The objectors used a verse to deny a truth of the unseen. Are there any unseen truths (like the barzakh life) that you are tempted to minimize because of a surface-level reading of certain verses? How will you cultivate deeper trust in the scholars' explanations?
?
This lesson shows that the dead in this world cannot hear, but in barzakh they do. Do you live as if you believe that those in the barzakh can hear your prayers? What would change in your daily supplications if you truly internalized that?

Life in the Grave: Refuting the Empirical Objection
Sanusi refutes the empirical objection by analogies and barzakh principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the empirical observation of dead bodies does not contradict the reality of life in the grave, based on Sanusi's refutation using analogies from revelation and the principle of barzakh.

Core Idea
What you see is not all there is — the grave hides realities beyond normal perception.

Sanusi tackles a common objection: we see dead bodies lifeless, so how can there be life in the grave? He responds by pointing out that many unseen realities, like the sleeper's internal experiences and the angels' invisibility, are accepted. This passage establishes the barzakh as a realm where normal laws are suspended, requiring belief in the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Sanusi's response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the bluntness of Sanusi's reply: such doubt indicates weak faith, akin to the disbelievers' denial of resurrection.
The objection is from those who trust only empirical evidence.
2
Examine the analogies
Identify the analogies: the Prophet seeing angels unseen by others, the succession of angels, the sleeper's internal experiences. These show that perceptibility is not a condition for reality.
The Quranic verse about Iblis being seen while we don't see him is key.
3
Understand the barzakh principle
The barzakh is the first stage of the afterlife where normal laws ('adat) are breached. This makes it possible for the deceased to experience life in the grave imperceptible to us.
Barzakh literally means barrier or intermediate realm.
4
Conclude with obligatory belief
Since the Shari'ah reports these miraculous events and they are rationally possible, belief in them is obligatory. The passage ends with a prayer.
This reinforces the importance of iman bil-ghayb (belief in the unseen).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نحن نرى من دفناه على حاله، ونعلم بالضرورة كونه ميتاً. قلنا: هذا يُؤذِنُ مِنْ قائِلِهِ بعدم طمأنينته إلى الإيمان! وهو بمثابة استبعاد الكفرة حشر العظام البالية، ومن يُسَلَّمُ اختصاص الرسول برؤية الملك دون القوم، وتعاقب الملائكة فينا، وقوله تعالى في إبليس وجنوده: ﴿ إِنَّهُ يَرَنكُمْ هُوَ وَقَبِيلُهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ لا ترونهم ﴾ [الأعراف: 27].. لا يشك في التصديق بذلك، كيف والنائم يدرك أحوالاً من السرور والغموم والآلام من نفسه، ونحن لا نشاهد ذلك منه؟! والبرزخ أول منزل من منازل الآخرة، وفيه تغيير العادات وخرقها، فيصح أن يكون الميت حال مشاهدتنا له، والقبر حال نظرنا إليه.. على غير الحالة التي نشاهدها ولم نشعر بشيء مما هنالك والأمر بيد الله تعالى، يُظهر ما يشاء، ويحجب ما يشاء. نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا ممن آمن به وملائكته وكتبه ورسله، ويختم لنا بخواتم السعداء، ويؤمن روعتنا في الدنيا والآخرة. ولا يقدح فيه مشاهدتنا للميت على نحو ما وضع في قبره؛ لأن في الموت وما بعده خوارق عادات أخبر بها الشرع، وهي جائزة، فوجب الإيمان بها على ظاهرها.
If they say: We see the one we buried in his state, and we know by necessity that he is dead. We say: This indicates from its speaker a lack of tranquility in faith! It is like the disbelievers' deeming remote the gathering of decayed bones. And is it not accepted that the Prophet is singled out for seeing the angel while his people are not, and the succession of angels among us, and His statement (Exalted) concerning Iblis and his hosts: 'Indeed, he sees you, he and his tribe, from where you do not see them' [Q. 7:27]? There is no doubt in affirming that. How is it that the sleeper experiences states of joy, sorrow, and pain from himself, while we do not witness that from him?! The barzakh is the first stage of the stages of the afterlife, and in it occurs the changing of customs and their breaching, so it is valid that the deceased while we are observing him, and the grave while we are looking at it, may be in a state different from what we observe, and we are not aware of anything of that. And the affair is in the hand of Allah (Exalted), He manifests what He wills and veils what He wills. We ask Him, glorified, to make us among those who believe in Him, His angels, His books, and His messengers, and to seal for us with the seals of the happy ones, and to secure our terror in this world and the Hereafter. And our observation of the deceased as he was placed in his grave does not impair [this belief], because in death and what follows there are miraculous breaches of custom that the Shari'ah informed of, and they are permissible, so it is obligatory to believe in them according to their apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage addresses the empirical objection: people see dead bodies remain lifeless, so how can there be life in the grave? Sanusi responds by first noting that such doubt indicates weak faith, resembling the disbelievers' denial of resurrection of bones. He then invokes analogies from revelation: angels seeing us unseen, the Prophet seeing angels while others do not, and the succession of angels. Even in everyday life, a sleeper may experience pain or joy without external signs. The barzakh (intermediate realm) is the first stage of the afterlife where normal laws are suspended. Therefore, it is completely possible that the deceased in the grave is in a state imperceptible to us. Allah has full power to show or conceal whatever He wills. The passage concludes with a prayer and affirmation that such beliefs are based on the Shari'ah's report of miraculous events, which are possible and must be taken literally.

Key Terms
برزخ
خرق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because dead bodies appear lifeless, there cannot be life in the grave.
Correction: The barzakh is a realm where normal laws are suspended; many realities (like angels, sleeper's experiences) are imperceptible to us.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to demonstrate that imperceptible states can be real despite lack of external evidence?
A) The example of a painter creating an image.
B) The example of a sleeper experiencing emotions not visible to observers.
C) The example of a stone falling.
D) The example of fire burning.

Key Takeaway
Life in the grave is a hidden reality of the barzakh, beyond empirical observation, affirmed by revelation and rational analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the graves of loved ones, and they appear still and silent. Do you truly believe that beneath that dust, they are experiencing joy or torment based on their deeds? What does your heart say—and what does your daily life prove about that belief? Will you let this lesson transform your indifference into urgent action: praying for them, preparing for your own journey into the unseen?
?
The sleeper feels joy and pain without anyone knowing—how often do you act as if only what is seen matters? Your hidden sins, your secret charities, your private moments of faith—they are all recorded and will shape your barzakh. Are you living as if that hidden world is more real than this visible one? What one change will you make today to ensure your unseen self is prepared for what awaits?
